The Significance of the Basmalah in Hanafi Fiqh
Opening invocation of the book

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the meaning of the basmalah and explain its status as a recommended (sunnah) invocation in Islamic practice, with particular reference to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The basmalah is a sunnah invocation for barakah, not a legal obligation.

The author begins with the basmalah, following the sunnah of the Prophet ﷺ and the practice of Muslim scholars. This lesson explores its meaning, legal status, and application in daily life according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the meaning
Understand the Arabic phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' which means 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.'
2
Recognize its legal status
Determine that reciting the basmalah before acts is sunnah (recommended), not fard or wajib. This is based on general proofs from the Quran and Sunnah.
Hanafis consider it sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah) for many acts.
3
Apply to daily acts
In Hanafi fiqh, the basmalah is recited before wudu, eating, slaughtering, and other permissible actions. It is also recited silently in prayer.
The basmalah is not recited aloud in prayer according to the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This is the opening invocation of the book. The author begins with the basmalah following the sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the practice of Muslim scholars. It is a statement of seeking blessing and help from Allah before commencing any work. In Hanafi fiqh, the basmalah is recited before many acts of worship and daily activities as a means of barakah.

Key Terms
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم (Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim): The opening phrase of the book, meaning 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling (hukm) of reciting the basmalah before performing wudu?
A) Fard (obligatory) – wudu is invalid without it.
B) Wajib (necessary) – compensation is required if omitted.
C) Sunnah (recommended) – it brings barakah but is not required.
D) Makruh (disliked) – it is not from the practice of the Salaf.

Key Takeaway
The basmalah is a sunnah invocation for seeking Allah's blessing and help; it is not a legal obligation.

beginner

The Ruling of Basmalah: Sunnah Muakkadah with Hadith Proof
Commentary on the basmalah, its ruling, and grammatical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the basmalah and explain the evidence from the hadith, including the meaning of 'ajzam' and the reconciliation with the hamdalah hadith.

Core Idea
The basmalah is sunnah muakkadah; the hadith's 'ajzam' indicates lack of blessing, not invalidity.

This passage establishes the ruling of the basmalah from a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The author resolves an apparent contradiction with another hadith and explains why the basmalah is recommended but not obligatory. We will walk through the proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is beginning important matters with basmalah. The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah).
The hadith is ahad, so it gives zanni proof.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Every important matter that does not begin with Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim is cut off.'
This is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'ajzam' means cut off; Hanafi interprets it as cut off from blessing, not invalid. So the action is still valid but lacks blessing.
Thus the ruling is sunnah, not wajib.
4
Resolve the conflict with the hamdalah hadith
Another hadith says 'Every important matter not beginning with al-Hamdulillah is maimed.' The author explains that basmalah is the real beginning and hamdalah is additional, so no contradiction.
The conjunction is omitted to avoid implying subordination.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is that basmalah is sunnah muakkadah before every lawful action.
This is consistent with the standard Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافتتحت الكتاب فقلت: ( بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم اقتداء بكتاب الله، وعملاً بقول سيدنا محمد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: « كل أمر ذي بال، لا يبدأُ فيه ببسم الله الرحمن الرحيم، فهو أجذم»، وفي رواية: ببسم الله فهو أبتر . ولا تعارض بينه وبين قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « كل أمر ذي بـال، لم يبدأ فيه بالحمد الله، فهو أجدمُ » (٢) أي: أقطع، لأن الابتداء بالأول حقيقي، وبالثاني إضافي لما اسواه، ولذلك ترك العاطف بينهما؛ لئلا يشعر بالتبعية فيخل بالتسوية، فعقب البسملة بالحَمْدَلة، والباء متعلقة بمحذوف تقديره: باسم الله أُأَلِفُ، وهو أولى من ابتدئ، إذ يضمر كــل فـاعـل فـعـلـه في ابتدائــه بالتسمية؛ كالمسافر إذا حل أو ارتحل فقال: بسم ا الله، كان المعنى باسم الله أحل، وباسم الله أرتحل. والاسم مشتق من السمو وهو العلو، وقيل: من الوسم وهو العلامة، وإنما حذفوا ألفه، وإن كان وضع الخط على حكم الابتداء، دون الدرج؛ لكثرة الاستعمال، وطولوا الباء لتكون كالعوض من الألف، ولا فتتاح كتاب الله تعالى بحرف معظم. وكان عمر بن عبد العزيز -رحمه الله تعالى- يقول لكتابه: طولوا الباء، وأظهروا السين، وفرقوا بينهما، ودورا الميم؛ تعظيماً لكتاب الله تعالى». وإنمـا قـدر المتعلق متأخراً، لأن ذكر الاسم أولاً أهم، وفيه مخالفة لما كانوا يبادرون بـه مـن أسماء آلهتهم، فوجب أن يقصد المُوَحّدُ معنى اختصاص اسم الله تعالى بالا بتداء، وذلك بتقديمه وتأخير الفعل كما في: ﴿إِيَّاكَ نَعْبُدُ وَإِيَّاكَ نَسْتَعِينُ ﴾ [الفاتحي: ٥]، وبسمِ اللهِ تجربها ) [هود: ٤١]، فقد أفـــاد التقديم اختصاصه به في كل أمر ذي بال» (۳) بجعله مبدأ له، من حيث أنه لا يُعتد بـه شـرعـاً، مـا لـم يُصدر به ولا يَردُ اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ [العلق : ١] لاقتضاء المقام تقديم الفعل؛ لأنه أمر بإيجاد القراءة؛ لأن القراءة هنا أهم من حيث أنه مقام تعليم، لأنه أول ما نزل إلى قوله تعالى: (الأكرم [ الخلق : ٣] كما في رواية البخاري، أو إلى قوله : مَالَ يعلم [العلق : ٥] كما في رواية غيره. أو لأن باسم ربك متعلق باقرأ الثاني، ومعنى اقرأ الأول: أوجد القراءة من غير اعتبار تعديته إلى مقروء، كما في، فلان يعطي. أي: يوجد الإعطاء، والباء، للملابسة، والظرف مستقر حـال مـن ضمير أبتدئ الكتاب، كما في، دخلت عليه بثياب السفر، أو الاستعانة، والظرف لغو، كما في كتبـت بالقلم. مـن اختار الأول: نظر إلى أنه أدخل في التعظيم، ومن اختار الثاني: نظر إلى أنه مشعر بأن الفعل لا يتم ما
And I opened the book and said: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful,' following the Book of Allah and acting on the saying of our master Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Every important matter that does not begin with Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim is cut off,' and in another narration: 'with Bismillah it is severed.' And there is no contradiction between it and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every important matter that does not begin with al-Hamdulillah is maimed,' meaning cut off, because the beginning with the first is real, and with the second is additional to others. Therefore, the conjunction is omitted between them to avoid implying subordination that would disturb equality. So the basmalah is followed by the hamdalah. The 'ba' is attached to an omitted particle whose implied meaning is: 'with the name of Allah I begin,' and this is better than 'I start,' for every doer implies his action at the beginning with the naming. For example, when a traveler alights or departs and says 'Bismillah,' the meaning is 'with the name of Allah I alight, and with the name of Allah I depart.' 'Al-Ism' is derived from 'al-sumu' meaning height, or from 'al-wasm' meaning mark. They omitted its alif, even though the script is on the rule of beginning, not elision, due to frequent use, and they elongated the 'ba' to compensate for the alif, and to open the Book of Allah Almighty with a glorified letter. 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, may Allah have mercy on him, used to say to his scribes: 'Lengthen the ba, show the sin, separate them, and round the mim, as a glorification of the Book of Allah Almighty.' And the attached particle is only postponed because mentioning the name first is more important, and in it is opposition to what they used to rush to of the names of their gods. So it is necessary that the monotheist intends the meaning of the special beginning of the name of Allah Almighty, by giving it precedence and delaying the verb, as in: 'You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help' [Surah al-Fatihah: 5] and 'With the name of Allah it sails' [Surah Hud: 41]. The precedence thus indicates its speciality in every important matter, making it a beginning, in the sense that it is not considered legally unless preceded by it. And it does not contradict 'Read in the name of your Lord who created' [Surah al-Alaq: 1] because the context requires the verb to be given precedence, as it is a command to create reading; because reading here is more important in that it is a teaching context, as it was the first to be revealed up to His saying 'al-Akram' [Surah al-Alaq: 3] in the Bukhari narration, or up to His saying 'what he did not know' [Surah al-Alaq: 5] in other narrations. Or because 'with the name of your Lord' is attached to the second 'read', and the meaning of the first 'read' is: create reading without considering its transitivity to something read, as in 'so-and-so gives', i.e., he creates giving. And the 'ba' is for accompaniment, and the adverb is settled as a state from the pronoun of 'I begin the book', as in 'I entered upon him with travel clothes', or for seeking help, and the adverb is superfluous, as in 'I wrote with the pen'. Whoever chose the first considered that it is more inclusive of glorification; whoever chose the second considered that it indicates that the action is not complete without it.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the basmalah (the phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim'). The author begins by stating that he opens his book with the basmalah, following the Qur'an and the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The hadith cited is: 'Every important matter that does not begin with Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim is cut off (ajzam)' and another variant says 'abtar' (severed). The author then addresses an apparent contradiction with another hadith that says 'Every important matter not beginning with al-Hamdulillah is maimed.' He resolves this by explaining that the first beginning (basmalah) is the real start, while the second (hamdalah) is additional, so there is no conflict; that is why the conjunction is omitted between the two hadiths. The author then provides a grammatical analysis: the 'ba' in 'Bismillah' is attached to an implied verb, the best estimation being 'u'allifu' (I begin) rather than 'abtadi' (I start). This is because any doer naturally implies his action when saying 'Bismillah'. He then explores the etymology of 'ism' (name), saying it is derived from 'sumu' (height) or 'wasm' (mark). The omission of the alif in the script is due to frequent use, and the elongation of the 'ba' compensates for that and honors the Book of Allah. He quotes a narration from 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz instructing scribes to elongate the 'ba', show the 'sin', separate them, and round the 'mim' as glorification. The author then discusses why the verb is postponed: because mentioning the name first is more important and opposes the polytheists' practice of starting with their gods' names. He illustrates with Quranic verses where the name is given precedence. He also explains why the verse 'Read in the name of your Lord' has the verb first: because the context of the first revelation requires emphasis on the act of reading itself. Finally, he discusses the function of the 'ba'—whether it is for accompaniment (with the adverb as a hal) or for seeking help (with the adverb as superfluous). He notes that the choice between these opinions depends on whether one emphasizes glorification (first opinion) or the inability of the action to be complete without it (second opinion).

Key Terms
بسم الله (Bismillah): The phrase 'In the name of Allah' with which one begins important actions, seeking blessing.
أجذم (ajzam): Cut off; meaning the action is deprived of blessing or completeness.
حمدلة (hamdalah): The phrase 'al-Hamdulillah' (praise be to Allah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on reciting the basmalah before beginning an important matter?
A) Wajib
B) Sunnah muakkadah
C) Fard
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
The basmalah is sunnah muakkadah; omitting it does not invalidate the action but deprives it of blessing.

Linguistic and Grammatical Analysis of Bismillah
Commentary on the basmalah, its ruling, and grammatical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the grammatical structure of Bismillah, including the implied verb, the function of the ba', and the reasoning behind the omission of alif and elongation of ba.

Core Idea
In Bismillah, the name of Allah is given precedence to emphasize His centrality, and the ba' is either for accompaniment or for seeking help.

The author provides a detailed linguistic analysis of the basmalah, examining its grammar, etymology, and script. This lesson unpacks the implied verb (u'allifu), the two opinions on the ba', and the reasons for the peculiarities in the script.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implied verb
The ba' is attached to an implied verb. The best estimation is 'u'allifu' (I begin) because every doer implies his action when starting with the name.
Alternative: 'abtadi' (I start) is less suitable because it is more generic.
2
Analyze the two opinions on the ba'
First: ba' is for accompaniment (al-mulabasah), with the adverb as a hal (state). Second: ba' is for seeking help (al-isti'anah), with the adverb as laghw (superfluous).
The first emphasizes glorification; the second emphasizes the necessity of the basmalah for the action's completion.
3
Examine the etymology of 'ism'
It is derived from 'sumu' (height) or 'wasm' (mark). The alif is omitted due to frequent use. The ba' is elongated as compensation and to glorify Allah's name.
The script reflects reverence.
4
Understand the precedence of the name
The name 'Allah' is placed before the verb to give it priority and to oppose polytheists who would start with the names of their gods.
This is similar to 'Iyyaka na'budu' where the object precedes the verb for emphasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الله فهو أبتر . ولا تعارض بينه وبين قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « كل أمر ذي بـال، لم يبدأ فيه بالحمد الله، فهو أجدمُ » (٢) أي: أقطع، لأن الابتداء بالأول حقيقي، وبالثاني إضافي لما اسواه، ولذلك ترك العاطف يُعتد بـه شـرعـاً، مـا لـم يُصدر به ولا يَردُ اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ [العلق : ١] لاقتضاء المقام تقديم الفعل؛ لأنه أمر بإيجاد القراءة؛ لأن القراءة هنا أهم من حيث أنه مقام تعليم، لأنه And I opened the book and said: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful,' following the Book of Allah and acting on the saying of our master Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Every important matter that does not begin with Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim is cut off,' and in another narration: 'with Bismillah it is severed.' And there is no contradiction between it and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every important matter that does not begin with al-Hamdulillah is maimed,' meaning cut off, because the beginning with the first is real, and with the second is additional to others. Therefore, the conjunction is omitted between them to avoid implying subordination that would disturb equality. So the basmalah is followed by the hamdalah. The 'ba' is attached to an omitted particle whose implied meaning is: 'with the name of Allah I begin,' and this is better than 'I start,' for every doer implies his action at the beginning with the naming. For example, when a traveler alights or departs and says 'Bismillah,' the meaning is 'with the name of Allah I alight, and with the name of Allah I depart.' 'Al-Ism' is derived from 'al-sumu' meaning height, or from 'al-wasm' meaning mark. They omitted its alif, even though the script is on the rule of beginning, not elision, due to frequent use, and they elongated the 'ba' to compensate for the alif, and to open the Book of Allah Almighty with a glorified letter. And the attached particle is only postponed because mentioning the name first is more important, and in it is opposition to what they used to rush to of the names of their gods. The precedence thus indicates its speciality in every important matter, making it a beginning, in the sense that it is not considered legally unless preceded by it. Whoever chose the first considered that it is more inclusive of glorification; whoever chose the second considered that it indicates that the action is not complete without it.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the basmalah (the phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim'). The author begins by stating that he opens his book with the basmalah, following the Qur'an and the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). He resolves this by explaining that the first beginning (basmalah) is the real start, while the second (hamdalah) is additional, so there is no conflict; that is why the conjunction is omitted between the two hadiths. The author then provides a grammatical analysis: the 'ba' in 'Bismillah' is attached to an implied verb, the best estimation being 'u'allifu' (I begin) rather than 'abtadi' (I start). This is because any doer naturally implies his action when saying 'Bismillah'. He then explores the etymology of 'ism' (name), saying it is derived from 'sumu' (height) or 'wasm' (mark). The omission of the alif in the script is due to frequent use, and the elongation of the 'ba' compensates for that and honors the Book of Allah. He quotes a narration from 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz instructing scribes to elongate the 'ba', show the 'sin', separate them, and round the 'mim' as glorification. The author then discusses why the verb is postponed: because mentioning the name first is more important and opposes the polytheists' practice of starting with their gods' names. He illustrates with Quranic verses where the name is given precedence. He also explains why the verse 'Read in the name of your Lord' has the verb first: because the context of the first revelation requires emphasis on the act of reading itself. Finally, he discusses the function of the 'ba'—whether it is for accompaniment (with the adverb as a hal) or for seeking help (with the adverb as superfluous). He notes that the choice between these opinions depends on whether one emphasizes glorification (first opinion) or the inability of the action to be complete without it (second opinion).

Key Terms
بسم الله (Bismillah): The phrase 'In the name of Allah' with which one begins important actions, seeking blessing.
الاسم (al-ism): Name; derived from sumu (height) or wasm (mark). Refers to the name of Allah.
الباء (al-ba): The Arabic letter ba, used as a preposition in Bismillah. Its function is debated: for accompaniment (mulabasah) or seeking help (isti'anah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the origin of the word 'ism' (name) in 'Bismillah'?
A) From 'samt' (silence)
B) From 'sumu' (height) or 'wasm' (mark)
C) From 'isma' (preservation)
D) From 'asama' (to listen)

Key Takeaway
The basmalah's grammatical structure emphasizes Allah's name and is rich in linguistic and scriptural significance.

Defining Hamd and Shukr: Linguistic and Technical Meanings
Defining hamd and shukr linguistically and technically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of hamd and shukr and identify their respective scopes (domain and object).

Core Idea
Hamd is praise by the tongue; shukr is gratitude by heart, tongue, and limbs — each has a different scope.

In the opening of Nur al-Idah, the commentator establishes the meanings of hamd and shukr. These terms are central to Islamic worship but are often confused. This lesson unpacks their definitions and clarifies how they overlap and differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the act of magnification
The passage begins by defining the action that indicates magnification of the Benefactor, including verbal, physical, and belief components. Note that action is stronger than speech in indicating gratitude.
2
Distinguish linguistic and technical meanings
Linguistic hamd is praise by the tongue for any excellence. Technical shukr is directing all of Allah's blessings to their intended purpose. Recognize that conventional hamd is synonymous with linguistic shukr.
3
Determine the domain (mawrid) and object (muta'allaq)
Linguistic hamd has the tongue as domain and includes blessings and other things as object. Linguistic shukr has a broader domain (heart, tongue, limbs) but object is only blessings. Thus hamd is more general in object, shukr more general in domain.
4
Recognize the importance of the distinction
Understanding these scopes helps in correctly applying the terms in worship, especially when reciting 'al-hamdu lillah'.

Source Text

والشكر اللغوي، ومن البين أن الحمد العرفي، والشكر اللغوي، متحدان وبين الشكرين عموم مطلق، كما . بين الحمد لغة أو اصطلاحاً، وبين الشكر اصطلاحاً، فظهر أن الشكر اصطلاحاً أخص من الثلاثة، وأن الحمد عرفاً، والشكر لغة متحدان، وأن بين الحمد لغة، والحمد اصطلاحاً، عموماً من وجه، كما أن بين ا الحمد لغة، والشكر لغة، عموماً من وجه أيضاً، وقد نظم ذلك مولانا شيخ الإسلام الشيخ علي الأجهوري»، المالكي، فقال رحمه الله تعالى وقد أملاني بلفظه: [ من البحر الطويل ] إذا نسبة للحمد والشكر رمتها بوجه له عقلُ اللبيب يُوالِفُ فشكر لدى عرف أخص جميعها وفي لغة للحمد عرفاً يرادف عموم لوجه في سواهن نسبة وذي نسب ست لمن هو عارف يعم انتهى. وقال ناظمها النسب الست، نسبة الحمد لغة إلى الثلاثة بعده، ونسبة الحمـد اصطلاحاً للاثنين بعده، ونسبة الشكر لغة للشكر اصطلاحاً، فالنسبة بين الشكر العرفي وغيره، العموم والخصوص المطلق، وتحت المطلق. وقد نظمتها أيضاً فقلت: [ من البحر الطويل ] ونسبة شكر ذي اصطلاح لغيره عموم مع الإطلاق والشكر غير ذا فنسبته للحمـــد عُرفـــا تــــرادف وفي غير ذا الوجهين فاحفظ فحبذا رحمه العقائد كذا في شرح حاشية الشيخ علي الأجهوري على عقيدته التي نظمها رحمه الله في والحمد لغة من شعب المدح؛ لأن المدح أعم منه، إذ هو الثناء على الخلال (۱) مطلقاً، اختيارية كانت أو غيرها، إذ قد يمدح الإنسان على صباحة وجهه، ورشاقة قده ) ، كما يمدح ببذل ماله، وعمله وشجاعته، والثاني دون الأول، فبينهما عموم مطلق، فكل حمد مدح، ولا قلــب، والحمد في الأصل، من المصادر التي تنصب بأفعال مضمرة، لا يكاد يستعمل معها الفعل. وقال الإمام « البيضاوي » الله تعالى: التعريف فيه للجنس، ومعناه: الإشارة إلى ما يعرفه كل أحد أن الحمـد مـا هو، أو للاستغراق، إذ الحمد في الحقيقة كله له، إذ ما من خير إلا وهو موليه بواسطة أو بغير واسطة. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا بِكُم مِّن نِعْمَةٍ فَمِنَ اللَّهِ ﴾ [ال: ٥٣] انتهى. وقيل غير ذلك كما هو معلوم. وجملة الحمـد الله خبرية لفظاً، إنشائية معنى لحصول الحمد بالتكلم بها مع الإذعان لمدلولها، ويجوز أن تكون موضوعة شرعاً للإنشاء، فالحمد مختص بالله، كما أفادته الجملة اختصاصاً حقيقياً عند أهل الحق، أهل السنة والجماعة، فلا فرد منه لغيره، واخترنا الحمد على المدح لما تلوناه، لأن الثناء على الله تعالى دائماً صادر بعد الإحسان، فإنك لا تقدر على أن تثني عليه إلا بتوفيق منه، وهو نعمة الله، أي: للذات الواجب الوجود، المستحق لجميع المحامد، ولم نَقُل للخالق أو الرازق، أو نحوهـمـا مـمـا قـد يوهم اختصاص الحمد بوصف دون وصف، تنبيهاً على أن الحمد واجب للذات، ويشمل الحمد على جميع النعم التي لا تحصى. والعبارة تقصر عن الإحاطة بإفرادها. قال تعالى: ﴿وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَتَ اللَّهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ﴾ [ الراقية : ٣٤]. ولهذا أشار بعض العارفين بقوله: [ من البحر الطويل ]
The action that indicates the magnification of the Benefactor, in that He is the Benefactor upon the one praising or others, so it includes speech by the tongue and action which is work by the limbs, by performing actions that indicate that, and belief by the heart that He is characterized by that. Some of the gnostics – from the accomplished Sufi masters, people of reality – said: And it is stronger by action than by speech, because actions that are effects of generosity, for example, indicate it with definitive indication, unlike speech whose indication is conventional, and its meaning may lag behind it. From this category is Allah's praise and lauding upon His own Self. That is because when He spread out the carpet of existence upon countless possibles, and placed upon it the tables of His generosity that have no end, He has unveiled the attributes of His perfection and manifested them with a definitive, preferential, infinite indication. For every atom of the atoms of existence indicates them, and expressions cannot conceive such indications. Hence he said: 'I cannot count Your praise; You are as You have praised Yourself.' This is linguistic shukr, for it is the action done to magnify the Benefactor. In al-Qamus: al-hamd is al-shukr, and it is recognition of beneficence and its spreading. Technical shukr is the servant directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him to that for which it was created, such as directing the sight to contemplate His creations, the hearing to His remembrance and what leads to His pleasure, and avoiding His prohibitions. So the domain of linguistic hamd is only the tongue, and its object is blessings and other things. The domain of linguistic shukr encompasses the tongue and other than it, and its object is only blessings. Thus hamd is more general in terms of object and more specific in terms of domain, and shukr is with the heart. Hence, their coincidence in praise by the tongue in return for beneficence becomes actual, and their separation in that hamd alone applies to description of knowledge and courage, and shukr alone applies to praise by the heart in return for beneficence. So between the two hamds there is general-specific in one aspect, and likewise between linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr. It is clear that conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are identical, and between the two shukrs there is absolute general-specific, as between hamd linguistically or terminologically and shukr terminologically. Thus it becomes clear that terminological shukr is the most specific of the three, and that conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are identical, and between linguistic hamd and terminological hamd there is general-specific in one aspect, as between linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr there is also general-specific in one aspect. Our master Shaykh al-Islam Shaykh 'Ali al-Ajhuri al-Maliki, may Allah have mercy on him, organized this in verse, saying: [from al-Bahr al-Tawil] 'When you seek the relationship between hamd and shukr / With a face that the intelligent mind will harmonize / Then shukr in technical usage is the most specific of all / And in linguistic usage it is synonymous with conventional hamd / General-specific in one aspect in the others / And these are six relationships for one who knows.' He ended. And the organizer said: The six relationships are: the relationship of linguistic hamd to the three after it, and the relationship of terminological hamd to the two after it, and the relationship of linguistic shukr to technical shukr. So the relationship between technical shukr and other than it is absolute general-specific, and under this are three relationships. The relationship between linguistic hamd and conventional hamd is general-specific in one aspect, and likewise between linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr is identity if the condition of reaching the one thanking is not considered; if considered, the relationship between them is absolute general-specific. I also organized it and said: [from al-Bahr al-Tawil] 'And the relationship of technical shukr to other than it / Is absolute general-specific, and shukr other than this / So its relationship to conventional hamd is synonymous / And in other than these two aspects, so memorize, for it is good.' May Allah have mercy on him. This is in the commentary of the marginalia of Shaykh 'Ali al-Ajhuri on his creed which he organized, may Allah have mercy on him, in the beliefs. And hamd linguistically is a branch of praise, because praise is more general than it, for it is commendation of characteristics absolutely, whether voluntary or otherwise. For a person may be praised for the beauty of his face and the slenderness of his stature, just as he is praised for spending his wealth, his work, and his courage. The second is not like the first. So between them is absolute general-specific: every hamd is praise, but not the reverse. And hamd originally is among the verbal nouns that govern with implied verbs; the verb is hardly ever used with them. Imam al-Baydawi said, may Allah be exalted: The definite article here is for the genus, meaning an indication to what everyone knows that hamd is, or for totality, for hamd in reality is all for Him, for there is no good except that He is the one who grants it, directly or indirectly. He said, exalted: {And whatever blessings you have are from Allah} [al-Nahl 53]. End. And other things have been said as known. The sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' is declarative in form, performative in meaning, because hamd is obtained by uttering it with conviction of its meaning. And it is permissible that it is placed by the Law for performative utterance. Thus hamd is specific to Allah, as the sentence indicates, truly specific according to the people of truth, Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, so no individual of it is for other than Him. And we chose hamd over praise for what we have recited, because praise of Allah always occurs after beneficence, for you cannot praise Him except by His facilitation, and that is a blessing from Allah. That is: for the Essence that is necessary existence, deserving of all praises. And we did not say 'for the Creator' or 'the Provider' or similar that might suggest that hamd is specific to one attribute over another, as a warning that hamd is obligatory for the Essence, and it includes praise for all blessings that cannot be counted. Words fall short of encompassing them individually. He said, exalted: {And if you count the blessings of Allah, you cannot enumerate them} [Ibrahim 34]. Hence some gnostics pointed out by saying: [from al-Bahr al-Tawil]
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a definition and relationship analysis of the terms 'hamd' (praise) and 'shukr' (thanks) as they are used in the Hanafi tradition, specifically in the context of the opening of Nur al-Idah. The commentator begins by defining the act that conveys magnification of the Benefactor, noting that it includes verbal, physical, and belief components. He quotes some gnostics to emphasize that actual action is a stronger indication of gratitude than mere speech, because actions have definitive evidentiary force whereas words are conventional. This leads to a discussion of Allah's own self-praise, which is manifested through creation. The passage then cites al-Qamus to define hamd as shukr, and elaborates that technical shukr is the servant's utilization of Allah's blessings for their intended purposes. The author meticulously distinguishes between linguistic and technical usages of hamd and shukr, showing that hamd linguistically has a broader scope (it can be for blessings or other qualities), while shukr linguistically applies only to blessings but encompasses actions of the heart and limbs. They then present the relationships: technical shukr is the most specific, while conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are synonymous. The relationships are summarized in verse by Shaykh Ali al-Ajhuri, and later by the commentator himself. The passage further addresses the relationship between hamd and madh (praise), stating that hamd is a subset of madh, as madh can be for any characteristic, voluntary or not, while hamd is specifically for voluntary actions. Finally, the commentator explains the grammatical and legal significance of the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah'—it is declarative in form but performative in meaning, signifying that hamd is due to Allah alone, for His essence, not just for specific attributes. The conclusion is that hamd is obligatory due to the infinite blessings of Allah.

Key Terms
الحمد (al-ḥamd): Linguistically: praise by the tongue for any excellence, whether it is a blessing or not. Technically: praise directed to Allah for His essence and all blessings, often synonymous with linguistic shukr.
الشكر (al-shukr): Linguistically: recognition and spreading of beneficence, done by the heart, tongue, and limbs. Technically: directing all of Allah's blessings to their intended purpose. The most specific term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the domain (mawrid) of linguistic hamd?
A) Heart, tongue, and limbs
B) Tongue only
C) Heart only
D) Tongue and heart

Key Takeaway
Hamd and shukr have overlapping but distinct scopes: hamd is general in object but specific to the tongue, while shukr is general in domain but specific to blessings.

The Six Relationships Between Hamd and Shukr
Analyzing the six relationships between hamd and shukr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six relationships between hamd and shukr as detailed by Shaykh Ali al-Ajhuri and explain which terms are synonymous.

Core Idea
Technical shukr is the most specific; conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are synonymous.

After defining the terms, the commentator presents a sophisticated analysis of how the various meanings of hamd and shukr relate to each other. These relationships are summarized in verse and are essential for understanding the precise use of these words in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six terms
The passage uses six distinct terms: linguistic hamd, conventional hamd, terminological hamd, linguistic shukr, conventional shukr (or technical shukr), and terminological shukr. Note that 'terminological' and 'technical' are used interchangeably.
Terminological hamd is the same as 'technical hamd' and is the most restricted.
2
Understand the relationships through the poetry
Shaykh Ali al-Ajhuri's verse states: 'shukr in technical usage is the most specific of all, and in linguistic usage it is synonymous with conventional hamd'. This gives two key relationships.
The verse also mentions general-specific relationships in the others.
3
List the six relationships
The commentator explains: relationship between linguistic hamd and the three after it (conventional hamd, terminological hamd, linguistic shukr); relationship between terminological hamd and the two after it (conventional shukr, terminological shukr); relationship between linguistic shukr and terminological shukr.
These are described as either absolute general-specific (عموم مطلق) or general-specific in one aspect (عموم من وجه).
4
Recognize the synonomy of conventional hamd and linguistic shukr
The commentator explicitly states that conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are identical (متحدان). This is a key finding.
5
Note the relationship with madh
The passage also discusses madh (praise for any characteristic, voluntary or not) and states that hamd is a subset of madh (every hamd is madh but not vice versa).

والشكر لغة متحدان، وأن بين الحمد لغة، والحمد اصطلاحاً، عموماً من وجه، كما أن بين ا الحمد لغة، والشكر لغة، عموماً من وجه أيضاً، وقد نظم ذلك مولانا شيخ الإسلام الشيخ علي الأجهوري»، المالكي، فقال رحمه الله تعالى وقد أملاني بلفظه: [ من البحر الطويل ] إذا نسبة للحمد والشكر رمتها بوجه له عقلُ اللبيب يُوالِفُ فشكر لدى عرف أخص جميعها وفي لغة للحمد عرفاً يرادف عموم لوجه في سواهن نسبة وذي نسب ست لمن هو عارف يعم انتهى. وقال ناظمها النسب الست، نسبة الحمد لغة إلى الثلاثة بعده، ونسبة الحمـد اصطلاحاً للاثنين بعده، ونسبة الشكر لغة للشكر اصطلاحاً، فالنسبة بين الشكر العرفي وغيره، العموم والخصوص المطلق، وتحت هذا ثلاث نسب والنسبة بين الحمد اللغوي، والحمد العرفي، العموم والخصوص الوجهي، وكذا بين الحمد اللغوي والشكر اللغوي فهي الاتحاد، إن لم يعتبر قيد الوصول إلى الشاكر، فإن اعتبرت النسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق. وقد نظمتها أيضاً فقلت: [ من البحر الطويل ] ونسبة شكر ذي اصطلاح لغيره عموم مع الإطلاق والشكر غير ذا فنسبته للحمـــد عُرفـــا تــــرادف وفي غير ذا الوجهين فاحفظ فحبذا رحمه العقائد كذا في شرح حاشية الشيخ علي الأجهوري على عقيدته التي نظمها رحمه الله في والحمد لغة من شعب المدح؛ لأن المدح أعم منه، إذ هو الثناء على الخلال (۱) مطلقاً، اختيارية كانت أو غيرها، إذ قد يمدح الإنسان على صباحة وجهه، ورشاقة قده ) ، كما يمدح ببذل ماله، وعمله وشجاعته، والثاني دون الأول، فبينهما عموم مطلق، فكل حمد مدح، ولا قلــب، والحمد في الأصل، من المصادر التي تنصب بأفعال مضمرة، لا يكاد يستعمل معها الفعل. وقال الإمام « البيضاوي » الله تعالى: التعريف فيه للجنس، ومعناه: الإشارة إلى ما يعرفه كل أحد أن الحمـد مـا هو، أو للاستغراق، إذ الحمد في الحقيقة كله له، إذ ما من خير إلا وهو موليه بواسطة أو بغير واسطة. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا بِكُم مِّن نِعْمَةٍ فَمِنَ اللَّهِ ﴾ [ال: ٥٣] انتهى. وقيل غير ذلك كما هو معلوم. وجملة الحمـد الله خبرية لفظاً، إنشائية معنى لحصول الحمد بالتكلم بها مع الإذعان لمدلولها، ويجوز أن تكون موضوعة شرعاً للإنشاء، فالحمد مختص بالله، كما أفادته الجملة اختصاصاً حقيقياً عند أهل الحق، أهل السنة والجماعة، فلا فرد منه لغيره، واخترنا الحمد على المدح لما تلوناه، لأن الثناء على الله تعالى دائماً صادر بعد الإحسان، فإنك لا تقدر على أن تثني عليه إلا بتوفيق منه، وهو نعمة الله، أي: للذات الواجب الوجود، المستحق لجميع المحامد، ولم نَقُل للخالق أو الرازق، أو نحوهـمـا مـمـا قـد يوهم اختصاص الحمد بوصف دون وصف، تنبيهاً على أن الحمد واجب للذات، ويشمل الحمد على جميع النعم التي لا تحصى. والعبارة تقصر عن الإحاطة بإفرادها. قال تعالى: ﴿وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَتَ اللَّهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ﴾ [ الراقية : ٣٤]. ولهذا أشار بعض العارفين بقوله: [ من البحر الطويل ]
The action that indicates the magnification of the Benefactor, in that He is the Benefactor upon the one praising or others, so it includes speech by the tongue and action which is work by the limbs, by performing actions that indicate that, and belief by the heart that He is characterized by that. Some of the gnostics – from the accomplished Sufi masters, people of reality – said: And it is stronger by action than by speech, because actions that are effects of generosity, for example, indicate it with definitive indication, unlike speech whose indication is conventional, and its meaning may lag behind it. From this category is Allah's praise and lauding upon His own Self. That is because when He spread out the carpet of existence upon countless possibles, and placed upon it the tables of His generosity that have no end, He has unveiled the attributes of His perfection and manifested them with a definitive, preferential, infinite indication. For every atom of the atoms of existence indicates them, and expressions cannot conceive such indications. Hence he said: 'I cannot count Your praise; You are as You have praised Yourself.' This is linguistic shukr, for it is the action done to magnify the Benefactor. In al-Qamus: al-hamd is al-shukr, and it is recognition of beneficence and its spreading. Technical shukr is the servant directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him to that for which it was created, such as directing the sight to contemplate His creations, the hearing to His remembrance and what leads to His pleasure, and avoiding His prohibitions. So the domain of linguistic hamd is only the tongue, and its object is blessings and other things. The domain of linguistic shukr encompasses the tongue and other than it, and its object is only blessings. Thus hamd is more general in terms of object and more specific in terms of domain, and shukr is with the heart. Hence, their coincidence in praise by the tongue in return for beneficence becomes actual, and their separation in that hamd alone applies to description of knowledge and courage, and shukr alone applies to praise by the heart in return for beneficence. So between the two hamds there is general-specific in one aspect, and likewise between linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr. It is clear that conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are identical, and between the two shukrs there is absolute general-specific, as between hamd linguistically or terminologically and shukr terminologically. Thus it becomes clear that terminological shukr is the most specific of the three, and that conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are identical, and between linguistic hamd and terminological hamd there is general-specific in one aspect, as between linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr there is also general-specific in one aspect. Our master Shaykh al-Islam Shaykh 'Ali al-Ajhuri al-Maliki, may Allah have mercy on him, organized this in verse, saying: [from al-Bahr al-Tawil] 'When you seek the relationship between hamd and shukr / With a face that the intelligent mind will harmonize / Then shukr in technical usage is the most specific of all / And in linguistic usage it is synonymous with conventional hamd / General-specific in one aspect in the others / And these are six relationships for one who knows.' He ended. And the organizer said: The six relationships are: the relationship of linguistic hamd to the three after it, and the relationship of terminological hamd to the two after it, and the relationship of linguistic shukr to technical shukr. So the relationship between technical shukr and other than it is absolute general-specific, and under this are three relationships. The relationship between linguistic hamd and conventional hamd is general-specific in one aspect, and likewise between linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr is identity if the condition of reaching the one thanking is not considered; if considered, the relationship between them is absolute general-specific. I also organized it and said: [from al-Bahr al-Tawil] 'And the relationship of technical shukr to other than it / Is absolute general-specific, and shukr other than this / So its relationship to conventional hamd is synonymous / And in other than these two aspects, so memorize, for it is good.' May Allah have mercy on him. This is in the commentary of the marginalia of Shaykh 'Ali al-Ajhuri on his creed which he organized, may Allah have mercy on him, in the beliefs. And hamd linguistically is a branch of praise, because praise is more general than it, for it is commendation of characteristics absolutely, whether voluntary or otherwise. For a person may be praised for the beauty of his face and the slenderness of his stature, just as he is praised for spending his wealth, his work, and his courage. The second is not like the first. So between them is absolute general-specific: every hamd is praise, but not the reverse. And hamd originally is among the verbal nouns that govern with implied verbs; the verb is hardly ever used with them. Imam al-Baydawi said, may Allah be exalted: The definite article here is for the genus, meaning an indication to what everyone knows that hamd is, or for totality, for hamd in reality is all for Him, for there is no good except that He is the one who grants it, directly or indirectly. He said, exalted: {And whatever blessings you have are from Allah} [al-Nahl 53]. End. And other things have been said as known. The sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' is declarative in form, performative in meaning, because hamd is obtained by uttering it with conviction of its meaning. And it is permissible that it is placed by the Law for performative utterance. Thus hamd is specific to Allah, as the sentence indicates, truly specific according to the people of truth, Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, so no individual of it is for other than Him. And we chose hamd over praise for what we have recited, because praise of Allah always occurs after beneficence, for you cannot praise Him except by His facilitation, and that is a blessing from Allah. That is: for the Essence that is necessary existence, deserving of all praises. And we did not say 'for the Creator' or 'the Provider' or similar that might suggest that hamd is specific to one attribute over another, as a warning that hamd is obligatory for the Essence, and it includes praise for all blessings that cannot be counted. Words fall short of encompassing them individually. He said, exalted: {And if you count the blessings of Allah, you cannot enumerate them} [Ibrahim 34]. Hence some gnostics pointed out by saying: [from al-Bahr al-Tawil]
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a definition and relationship analysis of the terms 'hamd' (praise) and 'shukr' (thanks) as they are used in the Hanafi tradition, specifically in the context of the opening of Nur al-Idah. The commentator begins by defining the act that conveys magnification of the Benefactor, noting that it includes verbal, physical, and belief components. He quotes some gnostics to emphasize that actual action is a stronger indication of gratitude than mere speech, because actions have definitive evidentiary force whereas words are conventional. This leads to a discussion of Allah's own self-praise, which is manifested through creation. The passage then cites al-Qamus to define hamd as shukr, and elaborates that technical shukr is the servant's utilization of Allah's blessings for their intended purposes. The author meticulously distinguishes between linguistic and technical usages of hamd and shukr, showing that hamd linguistically has a broader scope (it can be for blessings or other qualities), while shukr linguistically applies only to blessings but encompasses actions of the heart and limbs. They then present the relationships: technical shukr is the most specific, while conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are synonymous. The relationships are summarized in verse by Shaykh Ali al-Ajhuri, and later by the commentator himself. The passage further addresses the relationship between hamd and madh (praise), stating that hamd is a subset of madh, as madh can be for any characteristic, voluntary or not, while hamd is specifically for voluntary actions. Finally, the commentator explains the grammatical and legal significance of the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah'—it is declarative in form but performative in meaning, signifying that hamd is due to Allah alone, for His essence, not just for specific attributes. The conclusion is that hamd is obligatory due to the infinite blessings of Allah.

Key Terms
الحمد (al-ḥamd): Linguistically: praise by the tongue for any excellence. Technically: praise directed to Allah for His essence and all blessings, often synonymous with linguistic shukr.
الشكر (al-shukr): Linguistically: recognition and spreading of beneficence by heart, tongue, and limbs. Technically: directing all of Allah's blessings to their intended purpose. The most specific term.
المدح (al-madḥ): Praise for any characteristic, voluntary or involuntary, such as beauty or generosity. More general than hamd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following describes the relationship between technical shukr and other terms?
A) General-specific in one aspect
B) Absolute general-specific (technical shukr is more specific)
C) Synonymous
D) General-specific in one aspect with some, absolute with others

Key Takeaway
The six relationships reveal that technical shukr is the most specific, conventional hamd and linguistic shukr are synonymous, and all other relationships are either general-specific in one aspect or absolute general-specific.

Rulings on Salawat Upon the Prophet and His Family
Commentary on wording and ruling of salat and salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on including the family (آل) in salawat and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Including the Prophet's family in salawat is wajib; combining salat and salam is sunnah.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the wording and ruling of sending blessings and peace upon the Prophet (ﷺ). The author clarifies that while it is mustahabb to begin speech with salawat, including the family (آل) is wajib based on the hadith 'include comprehensively'. Let us examine the proofs and derive the rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon: sending salawat (blessings) and salam (peace) upon the Prophet and his family. According to the Hanafi school, including the family is wajib, while combining salat and salam is mustahabb (recommended).
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites three hadiths. The first two encourage salawat in general; the third ("When you send salutation, include comprehensively") is the key proof for including the family.
The hadiths are ahad (zanni al-thubut), so they establish obligation only if the command indicates wujub.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For the family inclusion hadith, the command 'include comprehensively' (فَعَمِّمُوا) is an imperative, which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is a contrary indicator.
The other hadiths use encouragement, not command, so they establish mustahabb.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars consider including the family to be sunnah, not wajib. The Hanafi school holds the stronger view (mufta bihi) that it is wajib based on the apparent command.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that one who omits the family is sinning according to Hanafis, but not according to others.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: A Hanafi must include the family in every salawat; omission is considered a sin. Other schools may consider it merely a loss of reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسَّلامُ عَلَى سَيِّدِنَا مُحَمَّدٍ حَاتَم النَّبِيِّينَ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ. وتعظيمه، بالألف مبدلة عن واو لفظاً، وبالواو كتابة، إلا إذا أضيف أو ثني، فقيل: صلاتك أو صلاتان، وقال « ابن درستويه»: لم يثبت بالواو في غير القرآن وهي: اسم من التصلية، أي: الثناء الكامل، ولما أن كان ليس في وسعنا سألناه من الله تعالى. (و) كذا (السلام)، وهـو : اسم من التسليم، وقيل: مصدر ثلاثي أو مزيد، والأول أصح. والمعنى: جعله الله تعالى سالماً عن كل مكروه، وحيَّاه بما يليق بجلاله وعظمته لشريف الجناب، وجمعنا بينهما امتثالاً للأمر بهما ، وإن لم يكره إفراد أحدهما ، لأنه الأكمل عندنا، ولقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: كل كلام لا يبدأ فيه بالصلاة علي فهو أقطعُ ممحوقٌ مِنْ كلّ بركة ، وقوله : « مَنْ صَلَّى عَلَيَّ في كتاب، لم تَزَلْ المَلائِكَةُ تستغفر له، ما دام اسمي في ذلك الكتاب» (۳). (عـلـى سـيـدنـا)، خبر للمعطوفين، وجاز أن تكون خبر الثاني، وخبر الأول محذوف عند سيبويه». وقيل: يجوز القلب. (محمد)، أشهر أسمائه الشريفة، وهي ألف اسم، وسمي بـه إلهامـاً، والمعنى: ذات كـثـر خـصالـه المحمودة، أو كثر الحمد له في الأرض والسماء، أو كثر حمده تعالى له (خاتم النبيين)، النبيء، بالهمز، من النبأ أي الخبر، وبلا همز، وهو الأكثر من النبوة، وهي الرفعة، لأن النبي مرفوع الرتبة، فمن الأول يكون فعيلاً بمعنى مُفْعِل، يعني: منبئ الأخبـار والـغـيـوب عــن الله تعالى، ولمـا كـانت الصلاة عليه غير تامة بدون الصلاة على الآل كما في شرح التأويلات»، عطفها. فقال: (وعلى آله). بألف مبدلة عن الهمزة المبدلة عن الهاء عند البصريين، وعن الواو عند الكوفيين، والأول أصح لغة. وإضافة الآل إلى الضمير قليلة، أو غير جائزة، والمختار الآل والأحسن آل محمد، ولكنه أضاف إليه اختصاراً، والآل يطلق فبالاشتراك اللفظي على ثلاثة معان، أحدها: الجند والأتباع، نحو آل فرعــــون. والثاني: النفس، نحو: آل موسى وآل هارون يعني : نفسهما، والثالث بمعنى أهل البيت خاصة، نحو: آل محمد . وإنما وجب ذكر آله أيضاً في الصلاة معه لقوله : ( إِذا صَلَّيتُمْ فَعَمِّمُوا ) . الطَّاهِرينَ، وَصَحَابَتِهِ أَجْمَعِينَ.
And peace be upon our master Muhammad, the seal of the prophets, and upon his family. And its magnification, with alif replaced from waw in pronunciation, and with waw in writing, except when annexed or dual, so one says: your prayer or two prayers. Ibn Dastuwayh said: It is not established with waw except in the Quran. And it (salat) is a noun derived from tasliyah, i.e., complete praise. Since it is beyond our capacity, we ask it from Allah Almighty. (And) likewise (al-salam), and it is a noun from taslim, and it is said: a triliteral or quadriliteral verbal noun, and the first is more correct. The meaning: May Allah make him safe from every disliked thing, and greet him with what befits His majesty and greatness for his noble rank. And we combine both in obedience to the command for them, although it is not disliked to mention one alone, because it is the most perfect in our view, and due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every speech that does not begin with salutation upon me is cut off, devoid of all blessing,' and his saying: 'Whoever sends salutation upon me in a book, the angels will continue to seek forgiveness for him as long as my name is in that book.' (Upon our master), predicate for the coordinated elements, and it is permissible for it to be the predicate of the second, and the predicate of the first is omitted according to Sibawayh. And it is said: it is permissible to invert. (Muhammad), the most famous of his noble names, and they are a thousand names, and he was named so by inspiration, and the meaning: one with many praiseworthy qualities, or much praise for him on earth and in heaven, or much praise of Allah for him. (Seal of the prophets), al-nabi, with hamzah, from naba meaning news, and without hamzah, which is more common, from nubuwwah, meaning elevation, because the prophet is elevated in rank. Thus from the first it is fa'il in the sense of muf'il, i.e., one who informs of news and unseen matters from Allah Almighty. And since salutation upon him is not complete without salutation upon the family, as in Sharh al-Ta'wilat, he coordinated it. So he said: (And upon his family). With alif replaced from hamzah which is replaced from ha according to the Basrans, and from waw according to the Kufans, and the first is correct linguistically. And annexing 'al' to the pronoun is rare or not permissible, and the chosen is 'al' and the best is 'Al Muhammad', but he annexed it for brevity. And 'al' is used by verbal homonymy for three meanings: first: army and followers, like the family of Pharaoh; second: self, like the family of Musa and family of Harun meaning themselves; third: in the sense of family specifically, like the family of Muhammad. And it is obligatory to mention his family also in the salutation with him due to his saying: 'When you send salutation, include comprehensively.' The pure ones and all his companions.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the wording and ruling of sending salat (blessings) and salam (peace) upon the Prophet (ﷺ) and his family. The author, al-Shurunbulali, explains the linguistic origins of 'salat' and 'salam'. He states that it is not disliked to mention only one of them, but combining both is more complete and better (afdal) in the Hanafi school. This is supported by two hadiths: (1) Any speech that does not begin with salawat upon the Prophet is devoid of blessing. (2) Whoever writes salawat upon the Prophet in a book, angels seek forgiveness for him as long as his name remains in that book. The ruling is that it is mustahabb (recommended) to begin with salawat. Furthermore, it is wajib (obligatory) to include the family (al) when sending salutations, as per the hadith 'When you send salutation, include comprehensively.' The term 'al' is polysemous and can mean followers, self, or family. In the context of the Prophet, it refers to his believing family and followers. The commentary then analyzes the grammatical structure and meanings of the names.

Key Terms
الصلاة (al-salat): Here it means supplication, praise, or blessing from Allah; specifically the phrase 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'.
السلام (al-salam): Peace, safety from all harm; a greeting of honor.
آل (al): Family, followers, or self; here it refers to the family of the Prophet (ﷺ) specifically the believers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on including the family (آل) when sending salawat upon the Prophet?
A) It is sunnah
B) It is wajib
C) It is mustahabb
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, salawat upon the Prophet must include his family (wajib), and combining salat and salam is sunnah; the proofs are from hadith.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Oath Regarding Laylat al-Qadr
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of an oath regarding Laylat al-Qadr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding an oath concerning Laylat al-Qadr and identify the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Core Idea
An oath regarding Laylat al-Qadr is broken when the night is certain — and that depends on whether the night is fixed or movable.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the timing of an oath concerning Laylat al-Qadr. The disagreement stems from differing views on the nature of Laylat al-Qadr: is it a fixed but unknown night, or does it shift each year? Understanding this nuance directly affects when an oath is considered broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and core dispute
The act is an oath (yamin) regarding Laylat al-Qadr. The dispute is about when the oath is broken (hinth).
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
He holds that the oath is broken only after all nights of the next Ramadan have passed.
This is the mufta bihi view.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
They hold that the oath is broken when the corresponding time in the next Ramadan arrives.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
4
Trace the underlying belief about Laylat al-Qadr
Abu Hanifa believes Laylat al-Qadr may shift (tataqaddam aw tata'akhkhar), while the Sahibayn believe it is a fixed but unknown night.
Both agree it is in Ramadan without dispute.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: the timing of the oath's expiration differs. Under Abu Hanifa, one must wait longer; under Sahibayn, it is sooner.
6
Note the relied-upon view
The text states 'wa alayhi al-fatwa' indicating that Abu Hanifa's view is the basis for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإحياء ليلتي العيدين، وليالي عشر ذي الحجة. عندهما أي: عند أبي يوسف ومحمد إن كان الحلف في نصف شهر رمضان فيمينـه إلى النصـف مـن رمضان القابل، وعنده أي: الإمام أبو حنيفة إلى أن يمضي كل ليالي رمضــان الـقـابـل وعـلـيـه الـفـتـوى، والاختلاف بناء على أن ليلة القدر في رمضان عندهم بلا خلاف، لكنه يقول: عسى تتقدم أو تتأخر، وعندهما في ليلة بعينها لا تتقدم ولا تتأخر، ولكن لا تعرف، فإذا جاء من رمضان القـابـل ذلـك الوقت الذي حلف فيه علم أنه جاء ليلة القدر فيحنث، انتهى.
And reviving the two nights of the two Eids, and the nights of the ten days of Dhul Hijjah. According to them two, meaning Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if the oath was in the middle of Ramadan, then his oath [extends] to the middle of the next Ramadan. According to him, meaning Imam Abu Hanifa: until all the nights of the next Ramadan have passed, and upon it is the fatwa. The difference is based on the fact that Laylat al-Qadr is in Ramadan according to them without dispute, but he (Abu Hanifa) says: it may come early or late. According to them (the two), it is a specific night that does not come early or late, but it is not known. So when from the next Ramadan that exact time at which he swore arrives, he knows that Laylat al-Qadr has come, so he breaks his oath. End.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath related to Laylat al-Qadr. The author states the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The disagreement arises from their views on the nature of Laylat al-Qadr. All agree that Laylat al-Qadr occurs in Ramadan. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it is not fixed on a specific night but may shift from year to year within Ramadan. Therefore, if a person swears an oath concerning Laylat al-Qadr, it is not fulfilled until all nights of the next Ramadan have passed, as the night could be any night. The two disciples (Sahibayn) maintain that Laylat al-Qadr is a specific, fixed night within Ramadan, but its identity remains unknown. Consequently, if a person swears in the middle of Ramadan, his oath expires when the corresponding time in the next Ramadan arrives, since that time coincides with the fixed night. The relied-upon position (al-fatwa) is that of Imam Abu Hanifa. The practical difference (thamarah) is that oaths concerning Laylat al-Qadr will be broken at different times depending on which view is followed.

Key Terms
ليلة القدر (Laylat al-Qadr): The Night of Decree, a night in Ramadan that is better than a thousand months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone swears an oath regarding Laylat al-Qadr in the middle of Ramadan, when is the oath broken?
A) At the middle of the next Ramadan
B) After all nights of the next Ramadan have passed
C) Immediately after the current Ramadan
D) The oath is never broken

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi view is that an oath concerning Laylat al-Qadr expires only after all nights of the next Ramadan have passed, due to the view that Laylat al-Qadr may shift.

The Legal Trick (Hila) for Insufficient Fidya
Describes a legal trick (hila) to fulfill deceased's obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal trick (makhlas) for fulfilling a deceased's obligations when the estate is insufficient, and describe the cyclical gift process with its conditions.

Core Idea
A repeated gift cycle allows a small payment to satisfy multiple obligations — a valid Hanafi legal device.

Sometimes a deceased's estate or a guardian's donation is too small to cover all missed prayers and fasts. The Hanafi school provides a practical exit: a cycle of giving and receiving that multiplies the value. This lesson unpacks the legal mechanism and its permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that the deceased's estate (or guardian's donation) is insufficient to cover all missed obligations (prayers, fasts, etc.). Determine that a legal exit (makhlas) is needed.
2
Outline the hila steps
The guardian gives a small amount (e.g., half a sa') to a poor person with the intention of dropping one obligation. The poor person then gifts it back to the guardian, who takes possession. This cycle is repeated until all obligations are theoretically fulfilled.
Each cycle must involve actual possession (qabd) by both parties for the gift to be valid.
3
Understand the legal basis
The hila relies on the principle that the obligation is to feed (it'am) once for each missed act. By repeatedly transferring ownership, the same food can 'feed' for multiple obligations. The Hanafi school permits this as a valid device.
This ruling comes from later fatwa works, not from zahir al-riwayah.
4
Note the required intention
At each giving, the guardian must intend to drop a specific obligation (e.g., one missed prayer or fast). The poor person's gift back is not intended for the deceased, only to enable the next cycle.
The intention (niyyah) is crucial for the cycle to be effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يَفِ ما أوصى به الميت عمَّا عليه، أو لم يوص بشيء وأراد الولي التبرع بما لا يفي بذلك عن الواجبات التي بيناها؛ يدفع ذلك المقدار للفقير بقصد إسقاط ما يريد عن الميت، فيسقط عن الميت بقَدْرِهِ، ثم بعد قبضه يهبه الفقير للولي ويقبضه لتتم الهبة وتملك، ثم يدفعه الولي للفقير بجهة الإسقاط، فيسقط عن الميت بقدره أيضاً، ثم يهبه الفقير للولي ويقبضه، ثم يدفعه الولي للفقير وهكذا يفعل مراراً حتى يسقط ما كان يظنه على الميت من صلاة وصيام ونحوهما مما ذكرناه من الواجبات، وهذا هو المخلص في ذلك إن شاء الله بمنّه وكرمه. (ويجوز إعطاءُ فِدْيَةِ صلوات)، وفدية أيام ونحوها (لواحدٍ) من الفقراء (جملة. بخلاف كفَّارة اليمين)، حيث لا يصح أن يدفع للواحد أكثر من نصف صاع في يوم، للنص على العدد فيها، (والله سبحانه وتعالى) الموفق بمنه وكرمه.
And if what the deceased willed does not suffice for what is upon him, or he did not will anything and the guardian wanted to donate something that does not suffice for the obligations we mentioned: he gives that amount to a poor person with the intention of dropping (the obligation) from the deceased, so it drops from the deceased to that extent. Then after the poor person takes possession, he gifts it back to the guardian and the guardian takes possession to complete the gift and ownership. Then the guardian gives it to the poor person with the intention of dropping (another obligation), so it drops from the deceased to that extent again. Then the poor person gifts it to the guardian and he takes possession, then the guardian gives it to the poor person, and so on repeatedly until what he thinks is upon the deceased from prayers, fasts, and similar obligations mentioned, drops. This is the legal exit (makhlas) in this matter, if Allah wills, by His grace. And it is permissible to give the fidya of prayers and fidya of days and the like to one poor person in a lump sum. Unlike the expiation of an oath, where it is not valid to give to one person more than half a sa' in a day, due to the text regarding the number. And Allah, the Exalted, is the One who grants success by His grace and generosity.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage provides a practical legal device (hila) for a situation where the deceased's estate (or the guardian's donation) is insufficient to cover all missed obligations (fasts, prayers, etc.). The trick involves a cycle of giving and receiving: the guardian gives a small amount (e.g., half a sa') to a poor person with the intention of satisfying one obligation. The poor person then gifts it back to the guardian, who gives it again for another obligation. This is repeated until all obligations are theoretically cleared. The text emphasizes that this is a valid makhlas (legal exit) and encourages hope in Allah's acceptance. Following that, the passage rules that it is permissible to combine the fidya for multiple prayers (or days) and give it to a single poor person in one lump sum. This is contrasted with the expiation for a broken oath (kaffarat al-yamin), where the text specifies ten poor people, so giving to one person more than half a sa' per day is not valid. The hila is a well-known Hanafi device to fulfill obligations without incurring extra burden, rooted in the principle that the obligation is to feed, not to repeat the physical act of worship.

Key Terms
مخلص (makhlas): A legal exit or device to satisfy obligations without extra expense, here through a repeated gift cycle.
هبة (hiba): Gift; in the hila, the poor person gifts back the received food to the guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for the hila to be valid?
A) The guardian must give a new amount each cycle.
B) The poor person must take actual possession (qabd) each time.
C) The poor person must be a relative of the deceased.
D) The guardian must intend to drop all obligations at once.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi makhlas (legal exit) uses repeated gifts of the same item to fulfill multiple obligations, provided proper possession and intention are maintained.

Lump Sum Fidya vs. Kaffarat al-Yamin
Distinguishes lump sum payment for fidya from strict distribution for kaffarat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that fidya for prayers and fasts can be given to one poor person in a lump sum, and distinguish it from the strict distribution required for kaffarat al-yamin.

Core Idea
Fidya can be lumped to one poor person; kaffarat must feed ten because the text specifies number.

The text draws a clear line: fideya for prayers and fasts can be given all to one person, but expiation for a broken oath must reach ten distinct recipients. The difference lies in the textual specification (nass) of 'ten' in kaffarat, which does not apply to fidya. This lesson examines the ruling and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Recognize that the passage discusses two distinct obligations: fidya (compensation for missed prayers or fasts) and kaffarat al-yamin (expiation for breaking an oath).
2
State the ruling for each
For fidya: it is permissible to give the entire amount to a single poor person in one lump sum (جملة). For kaffarat: it is not valid to give more than half a sa' to one person in a day; it must be distributed to ten different poor people.
3
Identify the basis of difference
The difference stems from the Quranic text for kaffarat: 'feeding ten poor people' (Quran 5:89). The number is specified, so the distribution must follow exactly (tawzi' 'ala al-a'dad). For fidya, there is no such textual specification of number; thus it can be summed up.
The passage explicitly says 'خلاف كفارة اليمين' and gives the reason 'للنص على العدد فيها' (due to the text regarding the number).
4
Apply to practice
A person who owes fidya for 60 missed fasts can give 60 sa' to one poor person. But a person who owes kaffarat for a broken oath must feed ten poor people, each with half a sa' (or equivalent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if what the deceased willed does not suffice for what is upon him, or he did not will anything and the guardian wanted to donate something that does not suffice for the obligations we mentioned: he gives that amount to a poor person with the intention of dropping (the obligation) from the deceased, so it drops from the deceased to that extent. Unlike the expiation of an oath, where it is not valid to give to one person more than half a sa' in a day, due to the text regarding the number. And Allah, the Exalted, is the One who grants success by His grace and generosity.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary

The trick involves a cycle of giving and receiving: the guardian gives a small amount (e.g., half a sa') to a poor person with the intention of satisfying one obligation. فدية (fidya): Compensation payment for missed obligatory fasts or prayers (e.g., feeding a poor person per missed day).
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): Expiation for breaking an oath, consisting of feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave.

Protection from Punishment of the Grave and Its Alleviation on Friday
Hadith and theological expansion on grave punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories protected from grave punishment according to the hadith and explain how punishment is alleviated on Friday for disbelievers and believers, distinguishing between 'adhab and daghṭah.

Core Idea
Friday's barakah lifts grave punishment even for disbelievers; only one hour of suffering for those who die on Friday.

This passage combines a prophetic hadith with theological commentary from Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. It teaches that the grave punishment is real, but Allah's mercy through Friday and Ramadan brings relief. We will unpack the three protected groups and the nuanced distinction between punishment and pressure for believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three protected categories
From the hadith, list the three types of people whom Allah protects from the punishment of the grave. Note that the muadhdhin and shahid are protected regardless of time, while the one who dies on Friday night is protected specifically due to the blessed time.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Abbas.
2
Distinguish between 'adhab and daghṭah
According to Abu al-Mu'in, obedient believers experience only pressure (daghṭah) in the grave, not punishment ('adhab). Disobedient believers suffer both. Understand the difference: 'adhab is punitive, daghṭah is a natural trauma of death.
Daghṭah is a form of constriction felt by all deceased.
3
Analyze the alleviation for disbelievers and believers
Disbelievers' continuous punishment is lifted on Friday and during Ramadan out of respect for the Prophet. For disobedient believers, punishment is suspended during Friday and Friday night, and it does not return until the Day of Resurrection.
This shows the special status of Friday in Islam.
4
Recognize the mercy for those who die on Friday
If a believer dies on Friday or Friday night, they suffer punishment and pressure for only one hour, then it is cut off permanently. This is a great mercy and encourages honoring Friday.
The passage cites Majma' al-Riwayat and al-Tatarkhaniyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن ابن عباس عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: « ثَلاثَةٌ يَعْصِمُهُمُ الله مِنْ عَذَابِ القَبْرِ المُؤَذِّنُ والشَّهِيدُ والمُتَوَفِّى فِي لَيْلَةَ الجُمُعَةِ » وقال أبو المعين في «أصوله» : قال أهل السنة والجماعة : عذاب القبر وسؤال منكر ونكير حق، لكن إذا كان كافراً فعذابه يدوم في القبر إلى يوم القيامة، ويُرفع عنهم العذاب يوم الجمعة، وشهر رمضان لحرمة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، ثم المؤمن على ضربين إن كان مطيعاً لا يكون له عذاب القبر، ويكون له ضغطة فيجد هول ذلك. وخوف رفه، لما أنه كان يتنعم بنعمة الله تعالى ولم يشكر النعمة، وإن كان عاصياً يكون له عذاب وضغطة القبر، لكن ينقطع عنه العذاب يوم الجمعة، وليلة الجمعة، ولا يعود العذاب إلى يوم القيامة، وإن مات يوم الجمعة أو ليلة الجمعة يكون له العذاب ساعة واحدة وضغطة، ثم يقطع عنه العذاب ولا يعود إلى يوم القيامة من مجمع الروايات» و« التاتارخانية».
And from Ibn Abbas from the Prophet: 'Three are protected by Allah from the punishment of the grave: the muadhdhin, the shahid (martyr), and the one who dies on the night of Friday.' And Abu al-Mu'in said in his Usul: Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: The punishment of the grave and the questioning of Munkar and Nakir are true. However, if the person is a disbeliever, his punishment continues in the grave until the Day of Resurrection, but punishment is lifted from them on Friday and the month of Ramadan due to the honor of the Prophet. Then the believer is of two types: if he is obedient, he has no punishment of the grave, but he has pressure (daghṭah) and feels its terror and fear of ease, because he was enjoying the blessings of Allah and did not thank the blessing. And if he is disobedient, he has punishment and pressure of the grave, but the punishment is cut off from him on Friday and Friday night, and the punishment does not return until the Day of Resurrection. And if he dies on Friday or Friday night, he has punishment for one hour and pressure, then the punishment is cut off and does not return until the Day of Resurrection. From Majma' al-Riwayat and al-Tatarkhaniyya.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith listing three categories of people whom Allah protects from the punishment of the grave: the muadhdhin (caller to prayer), the shahid (martyr), and the one who dies on the night of Friday. It then provides theological elaboration from Abu al-Mu'in: The punishment of the grave and the questioning by the angels Munkar and Nakir are established truths according to Ahl al-Sunnah. For disbelievers, punishment is continuous but is lifted on Friday and in Ramadan out of respect for the Prophet. Believers are divided into two types: the obedient believer has no actual punishment but experiences a pressure (daghṭah) and fear of accountability. The disobedient believer undergoes punishment in the grave, but it is suspended on Friday and Friday night, and if he dies on Friday or its night, he only suffers for one hour. This reflects the mercy of Allah on this blessed day.

Key Terms
عَذَاب القَبْر ('adhāb al-qabr): Punishment in the grave after death for disbelievers and sinful Muslims.
ضَغْطَة القَبْر (daghṭat al-qabr): The pressure or constriction felt by all deceased in the grave, even obedient believers, due to death's trauma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Mu'in, what happens to a disobedient believer's punishment in the grave on Friday?
A) It increases as a reminder of sin.
B) It is cut off and does not return until the Day of Resurrection.
C) It remains the same as any other day.
D) It is converted into pressure only.

Key Takeaway
The punishment of the grave is real, but Friday's blessing brings alleviation for all, and dying on Friday grants near-complete immunity.
