Permissible and Disliked Actions of the Mu'takif
Explains permissible and disliked actions for a mu'takif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible and disliked actions for a mu'takif according to the Hanafi school and identify the consequence of exiting the mosque for non-essential needs.

Core Idea
A mu'takif must perform all daily needs inside the mosque; exiting invalidates i'tikaf unless for unavoidable necessity.

This passage details the scope of a mu'takif's actions during seclusion. While essential needs like eating, drinking, and necessary sales are permitted within the mosque, engaging in trade or bringing sold items is disliked. Even permissible speech is disapproved if it distracts from worship. Understanding these rulings ensures the spiritual purpose of i'tikaf is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential needs permitted in the mosque
List the actions explicitly allowed: eating, drinking, sleeping, and contracting a sale for personal or family necessities. Note that these must be done inside the mosque.
The ruling is based on necessity; the mu'takif should not leave for these.
2
Recognize the disliked actions
Identify what is makruh: bringing the sold item into the mosque, contracting for trade, remaining silent if considered worship, and excessive speech without benefit. Understand why each is disliked (e.g., trade distracts from devotion).
Silence is only disliked if believed to be an act of worship; otherwise, it is permissible.
3
Determine the consequence of exiting the mosque
If a mu'takif leaves the mosque for these needs, his i'tikaf becomes invalid. Exceptions exist for urgent necessities like using the toilet when no one can bring aid.
Exiting to ascend the minaret for the call to prayer does not nullify i'tikaf.

وانتهى به غيره. وأكلُ المعتكف ، وشُرْبُهُ، ونومُهُ، وعَقْدُهُ البيعَ لِمَا يحتاجُهُ لنفسِهِ أَوْ عيالِهِ، في المسجدِ. وكرة إحضار المبيع فيه، وكُرهَ عَقْدُ ما كان للتجارة، وكره الصمتُ إن اعتقدَهُ قُرْبَةٌ، وَحَرُمَ الوَطْهُ وَدَوَاعِيهِ فسد وإلا فلا، ولا يفسد الخروج لصعود المنارة )، سواء كان مؤذناً أو غيره في الصحيح، ولـــو كــان بابها من خارج المسجد في ظاهر الرواية عن أبي حنيفة، كما في ( البرهان» (وانتـهـى بـه) أي: بالخروج (غيره) أي: غير الواجب من الاعتكاف النفل، فيكون غاية له لا يفسد به، إذ ليس للنفل حد مخصوص وأَكْلُ المعتكف، وشُرْبُهُ، ونومُهُ، وعَقْدُهُ البيعَ لِمَا يحتاجه لنفسه أو عياله) لا يكون إلا (في المسجد)؛ لضرورة الاعتكاف، ولا يخرج لهذه الأشياء حتى لو خرج لأجلها يفسد اعتكافه، إذ ليس في تقضي هذه الحاجات ما ينافي المسجد، وفي الظهيرية»: وقيل: يخرج بعـد الغروب للأكل والشرب انتهى قال صاحب ( البحر » : وينبغي حمله على ما إذا لم يجد من يأتي لــه به، فحينئذ يكون من الحوائج الضرورية كالبول انتهى، وفيه تأمل. (وكُرة إحضار المبيع فيه)؛ لأن المسجد محرز عن حقوق العباد، وفيه شغله بها وجعله كالدكان، (وكُرِهَ عَقْدُ ما كان للتجارة) لأنـــه منقطع إلى الله تعالى، فلا يشتغل بأمور الدنيا، ولهذا كره الخياطة ونحوها فيه، وكره لغير المعتكف . والمسجد أفردت أحكامه بباب مستقل. وكُره الصَّمتُ إن اعتقدَهُ قُرْبَةٌ)؛ لأنه منهي وهو صوم أهل الكتاب وقد نسخ ، وأما إذا لم يعتقد القربة فيه، ولكنه حفظ لسانه عن النطق مطلقاً. البيع عنه بما لا يفيد فلا بأس به، ولكنه يلازم قراءة القرآن والذكر والحديث، والعلم والتدريس وسير النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وقصص الأنبياء عليهم السلام وحكايات الصالحين وكتابة أمور الدين، وأما التكلـم بـغـير خير فلا يجوز لغير المعتكف (۳)، والكلام المباح مكروه يأكل الحسنات كما تأكل النار الحطب)، إذا جلس في المسجد ابتداء لذلك كما حققناه بالحاشية، (وَحَرُمَ الوَطْهُ وَدَوَاعِيَهُ) لقوله تعالى: وَلَا تُبَشِرُوهُنَّ وَأَنتُمْ عَكِفُونَ فِي الْمَسَاجِدِ﴾ [البقة : ۱۸۷]، فالتحق به دواعيه وهي كاللمس والقبلة لأن الجماع محظور فيه فيتعدى إلى دواعيه، كما في الإحرام والظهار والاستبراء، بخلاف الصوم لأن الكف عن الجماع هو الركن فيه، والخطر يثبت ضمناً كي لا يفوت الركن، فلم يتعد إلى دواعيه لأن ما ثبت بالضرورة يقدر بقدرها . (وبَطَلَ) الاعتكاف (بوَطْئِهِ وبالإنزال بدواعيه)، سواء كان عامداً أو ناسياً أو مكرهاً ليلاً أو نهاراً؛ لأنه محظور بالنص (۲) وله حالة مذكرة كحالة الصلاة والحج، بخلاف الصوم، ولو أمني بالتفكر أو بالنظر لا يفسد اعتكافه.
And it ends with something other than it. And the eating of the mu'takif, his drinking, his sleeping, his contracting a sale for what he needs for himself or his dependents, (all this) in the mosque. And it is disliked to bring the sold item into it, and it is disliked to contract for trade, and silence is disliked if he considers it a worship, and intercourse and its precursors are forbidden; if (intercourse occurs) it becomes invalid, otherwise not. And exiting to ascend the minaret does not invalidate, whether he is a muezzin or otherwise in the correct view, even if its door is outside the mosque in the apparent transmission from Abu Hanifa, as in Al-Burhan. And it ends with it (the exit) that which is other than obligatory from the supererogatory i'tikaf, so it becomes a limit for it and does not invalidate it, because the supererogatory has no specified limit. And the eating of the mu'takif, his drinking, his sleeping, his contracting a sale for what he needs for himself or his dependents, is only (in the mosque) due to the necessity of i'tikaf, and he does not go out for these things; if he goes out for them, his i'tikaf becomes invalid, because there is nothing in fulfilling these needs that contradicts (being in) the mosque. And in Al-Zahiriyya: it is said he goes out after sunset for eating and drinking, end quote. The author of Al-Bahr said: it should be interpreted as when he cannot find someone to bring it to him; then it becomes from the necessary needs like urinating, end quote, but there is deliberation. And it is disliked to bring the sold item into it (the mosque) because the mosque is protected from the rights of people, and it involves occupying it and making it like a shop. And it is disliked to contract for trade because he is devoted to Allah Almighty, so he should not occupy himself with worldly matters; for this reason, sewing and the like are disliked in it, and it is disliked for non-mu'takif as well. And the rulings of the mosque are given a separate chapter. And silence is disliked if he considers it worship because it is prohibited and it is the fast of the People of the Book and has been abrogated. But if he does not consider it worship but merely guards his tongue from speaking absolutely, there is no harm. But he should persist in reciting Quran, remembrance, hadith, knowledge, teaching, the biography of the Prophet (peace be upon him), stories of the prophets (peace be upon them), tales of the righteous, and writing religious matters. As for speaking without good, it is not permissible for a non-mu'takif, and permissible speech is disliked, it consumes good deeds like fire consumes firewood, if he sits in the mosque initially for that purpose as we have verified in the gloss. And intercourse and its precursors are forbidden due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And do not have relations with them while you are secluded in the mosques' [Al-Baqarah: 187]. So its precursors are attached to it, which are like touching and kissing, because coitus is prohibited in it, so it extends to its precursors, as in ihram, zihar, and istibra', unlike fasting, because refraining from coitus is the pillar in it, and the danger is established implicitly so that the pillar is not lost, so it did not extend to its precursors because what is established by necessity is measured by its extent. And i'tikaf becomes null by his intercourse and by ejaculation due to its precursors, whether intentionally, forgetfully, or under compulsion, by night or by day, because it is prohibited by the text and it has a state analogous to the state of prayer and Hajj, unlike fasting. And if he has a wet dream due to thinking or looking, his i'tikaf is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage, from a commentary on Nur al-Idah, explains the detailed rulings concerning a mu'takif's actions in the mosque. The author begins by stating that a mu'takif must perform all his ordinary needs – eating, drinking, sleeping, and even contracting necessary sales – within the mosque. Leaving the mosque for these purposes invalidates his i'tikaf unless it is for an urgent necessity like using the toilet when no one can bring him what he needs. The passage then discusses disliked actions: bringing a sold item into the mosque (as it treats the mosque like a shop), engaging in trade contracts, and remaining silent if one believes silence itself is an act of worship (since this resembles the practice of monks and was abrogated in Islam). However, remaining silent simply to guard one's tongue is permissible. The commentary then addresses the prohibition of sexual intercourse and its precursors (kissing, touching). This is proven by the Quranic verse from Al-Baqarah 2:187. The prohibition extends to precursors because the main act (intercourse) is forbidden, and in other contexts like ihram and zihar, the precursors are also forbidden. The passage contrasts this with fasting, where only intercourse nullifies the fast, not its precursors, because the essence of fasting is abstaining from those acts. Finally, the passage states that i'tikaf is nullified by intercourse and by ejaculation resulting from precursors, regardless of intent, memory, or coercion. The nullification applies to both night and day because the prohibition is explicit. However, if a man experiences a wet dream merely from thinking or looking (without physical contact), his i'tikaf remains valid.

Key Terms
المعتكف (al-mu'takif): The person performing i'tikaf, seclusion in a mosque for worship.
دواعي الوطء (dawa'ee al-wat'): Precursors to intercourse like kissing and touching.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a disliked (makruh) action for a mu'takif?
A) Eating inside the mosque
B) Contracting a sale for personal groceries
C) Bringing the purchased item into the mosque
D) Sleeping inside the mosque

Key Takeaway
A mu'takif must perform all necessary activities inside the mosque, and must avoid trade and bringing goods into the mosque to preserve its sanctity.

Prohibition of Intercourse and Nullification of I'tikaf
Explains prohibition of intercourse/precursors, proof, nullification, and exception for wet dream.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic proof for prohibiting intercourse and its precursors in i'tikaf, and distinguish between acts that nullify i'tikaf and those that do not.

Core Idea
Intercourse and its precursors nullify i'tikaf due to explicit Quranic prohibition, but a wet dream from thought or looking does not.

This lesson focuses on the clear prohibition of sexual intercourse and its precursors during i'tikaf, supported by Quran 2:187. The Hanafi school extends the prohibition to precursors like touching and kissing through qiyas to ihram. Importantly, nullification occurs only with actual intercourse or ejaculation from physical contact, not from mere imagination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic verse and its legal implication
The verse 'And do not have relations with them while you are secluded in the mosques' provides a direct prohibition. Understand that the Arabic term 'tubashiroohunna' includes both intercourse and its precursors in the Hanafi interpretation.
The prohibition is categorical, applying to all mu'takifs regardless of gender.
2
Understand the extension to precursors via qiyas
The Hanafi school analogizes i'tikaf to ihram and zihar, where precursors are also prohibited because the main act is forbidden. Contrast this with fasting, where precursors are not prohibited because the essence of fasting is abstaining from these acts.
The reasoning is based on the principle that what leads to the haram is itself haram.
3
Determine what nullifies i'tikaf
Intercourse and ejaculation resulting from physical precursors nullify i'tikaf, whether intentional, forgetful, or coerced. A wet dream from mere thought or looking does not nullify because it lacks direct physical cause.
Nullification applies equally to obligatory and supererogatory i'tikaf.

Inclusion of Nights in Vows of I'tikaf
Details the effect of vowing days or nights on i'tikaf obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether vowing days of i'tikaf automatically includes the nights, and apply the rule of explicit exception for a full month.

Core Idea
Vowing days of i'tikaf automatically includes nights unless the vow is for a full month with explicit exception.

Vows (nadhr) make an act of worship obligatory, but the scope can be ambiguous. This passage from the Hanafi commentary clarifies how to determine the required period when someone vows i'tikaf for days or nights. Understanding this helps ensure the vow is fulfilled correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on the effect of vowing i'tikaf for days or nights. The hukm is that the vow becomes wajib and must be fulfilled according to the rules that follow.
The vow itself makes the act wajib.
2
Analyze the default inclusion of nights when vowing days
Note that vowing 'days' (plural) automatically obligates the corresponding nights. This is based on the continuous nature of i'tikaf.
The plural 'days' encompasses the entire period including nights.
3
Analyze the reverse: vowing nights includes days
If someone vows 'nights' (plural), the days are also obligated and must be observed consecutively, even if not stipulated. This is the well-known transmission (zahir al-riwayah).
Consecutiveness is implied even without explicit condition.
4
Understand the exception for a full month
For a vow of a month (specified or unspecified), intention alone to exclude nights is not valid. The mu'takif must explicitly say 'except the nights' to avoid their inclusion.
This prevents incomplete fulfillment of a lengthy vow.
5
Apply the ruling to common scenarios
Practice applying: e.g., vowing two days obligates two nights; vowing one day obligates one night (the night before).
The principle is that i'tikaf spans day and night.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولزمته الليالي أيضاً بنذر اعتكاف أيام. ولزمته الأيام بنذرِ الليالي متتابعة، وإن لم يُشترط التتابع، في ظاهر الرواية. ولزمته ليلتان بنذر يومين، وصح ليه النهر خاصة، دونَ الليالي. وإن نذر اعتكاف شهر معين أو غير معين، ونوى الشهر خاصة، أو الليالي خاصة، لا تَعْمَلُ نَيْتُهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُصَرْحَ بالاستثناء، والاعتكاف مشروع بالكتاب.
And the nights are also binding upon him by vowing i'tikaf of days. And days are binding upon him by vowing nights consecutively, even if consecutiveness is not stipulated, in the apparent transmission. And two nights are binding upon him by vowing two days. And his intention for the day specifically is valid, without the nights. And if he vows i'tikaf of a month, specified or unspecified, and intends the day specifically or the nights specifically, his intention does not apply unless he explicitly makes an exception. And i'tikaf is legislated by the Book.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage, from the same commentary, explains the legal consequences of making a vow (nadhr) to perform i'tikaf for a certain number of days or nights. The author begins by stating that if someone vows to perform i'tikaf for some days (in the plural), the nights corresponding to those days are also included and binding. Conversely, if he vows nights (plural), the days are included and must be observed consecutively, even if he did not explicitly stipulate consecutiveness; this is the well-known transmission (zahir al-riwayah). The reason is that i'tikaf inherently continues through both day and night, unlike fasting which is only during the day. The passage then notes that vowing two days requires two nights (the preceding and following nights). If someone vows i'tikaf but intends only the daytime, his intention is valid only if he has not vowed a full month; for a month, the intention alone does not excuse the nights unless he explicitly says 'except the nights.' Finally, the passage closes by affirming that i'tikaf is established by the Quran (reference to the verse from Al-Baqarah).

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, making it obligatory to fulfill.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmission within the Hanafi school, generally from Abu Hanifa's direct students.
ليالي (layali): Nights; in this context, the nights corresponding to the vowed days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows to perform i'tikaf for 'two days' without any further specification, how many nights must he actually be in the mosque according to the Hanafi school?
A) None; only the two days are required.
B) One night (the night between the two days).
C) Two nights (the nights before and after the first day).
D) The nights are optional and not binding.

Key Takeaway
Vowing days of i'tikaf automatically obligates the corresponding nights in the Hanafi school, but a full month's vow requires explicit exception to exclude nights.

Binding of Nights and Days in Vowed I'tikaf
How vowing days or nights affects the obligation of the other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what becomes obligated when vowing i'tikaf for days or nights, and explain the reason based on the inherent continuity of i'tikaf.

Core Idea
Vowing days binds nights; vowing nights binds days consecutively — i'tikaf is inherently continuous.

When a person makes a vow (nadhr) to perform i'tikaf for a certain number of days or nights, a question arises: does the vow also obligate the other part of the day? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that the vow of days obligates the nights and vice versa, based on the principle that i'tikaf is a continuous act. The reasoning is rooted in the nature of i'tikaf itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Recognize that the act is a vow (nadhr) to perform i'tikaf, which is a form of worship. The ruling concerns the obligation that arises from the specific terms of the vow.
2
State the ruling for vowing days
If one vows to perform i'tikaf for a number of days (e.g., three days), the corresponding nights are also obligated. The plural 'days' includes the associated nights.
Entry must occur before sunset of the first night, and exit after sunset of the last day.
3
State the ruling for vowing nights
If one vows to perform i'tikaf for nights, the days are obligated consecutively, even if continuity was not stipulated. This is based on the inherent continuity of i'tikaf.
This applies according to the well-known transmission (zāhir al-riwāyah).
4
Identify the rationale (talil)
The foundation of i'tikaf is continuity (tawātu‘). Therefore, what is inherently connected (like days and nights) cannot be separated without explicit specification.
The dual (e.g., two days) is treated like the plural, so vowing two days obligates two nights.
5
Apply to the dual case
If one vows two days, two nights are obligated. This is derived by analogy from the plural rule and as a precaution.
The entry is at sunset of the first night.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَزِمَتْهُ الليالي أيضاً بنَدْرِ اعتكاف أيام. ولزمته الأيام بنذرِ الليالي متتابعة، وإن لم يُشترط التتابع، في ظاهر الرواية. ولزمته ليلتان بنذر يومين. (ولزمته الليالي أيضاً) أي: كما لزمته الأيـام (بنذر اعتكاف أيام)؛ لأن ذكر الأيام بلفظ الجمع يدخل فيه ما بإزائها من الليالي، وتدخل الليلـة الأولى فيدخــل المسجد قبل غروب الشمس من أول ليلة، ويخرج منه بعد غروبها من آخر أيامه. ولزمته الأيـام بنذرِ اللَّيالي مُتَتَابِعَةٌ، وإنْ لم يُشْتَرَطِ التّتابعُ، في ظاهرِ الرِّوايةِ) لأن مبنى الاعتكاف عـلـى التتابع، وتأثيره (۳) أن ما كان متفرقاً في نفسه لا يجب الوصول فيه إلا بالتنصيص، وما كان متصل الأجزاء لا يجوز تفريقه إلا بالتنصيص، ولزمته ليلتانِ بِنَذر يومين فيدخل عنـد الـغـروب، كمـا ذكـرنـا لأن المثنى في معنى الجمع فيلحق به هنا احتياطاً.
He is also obligated the nights by vowing days of i'tikaf. He is obligated the days consecutively by vowing the nights, even if continuity is not stipulated, according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah). He is obligated two nights by vowing two days. (He is also obligated the nights) i.e. just as he is obligated the days (by vowing days of i'tikaf); because mentioning 'days' in the plural includes their corresponding nights, and the first night is included so he enters the mosque before sunset of the first night and leaves after sunset of the last of his days. He is obligated the days consecutively by vowing the nights, even if continuity is not stipulated, in the apparent transmission, because the foundation of i'tikaf is on continuity, and its effect is that what is inherently separate does not require connection except by specification, and what is inherently connected cannot be separated except by specification. He is obligated two nights by vowing two days, so he enters at sunset, as we mentioned, because the dual is in the meaning of the plural and is thus annexed here as a precaution.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on what becomes obligatory when a person vows i'tikaf for a certain number of days or nights. If one vows days, the nights of those days are also obligated, meaning he must spend the entire 24-hour periods in the mosque, including the nights. He must enter before sunset of the first day's night. Conversely, if one vows nights, the days are obligated consecutively, even if he did not stipulate continuity. This is based on zahir al-riwayah, the established transmission within the school. The reasoning is that i'tikaf by nature is continuous (al-mabna 'ala al-tawatu'), so nights and days are connected. For vowing two days, he is obligated two nights (the night before and the night after? Actually he enters at sunset and stays through two days and two nights). The dual is treated like plural for this rule.

Key Terms
تتابع (tawātu’): Consecutiveness/continuity; here it means that i'tikaf should be performed without interruption.
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāyah): The well-known and accepted transmission within the Hanafi school, as opposed to rare reports.
مثنى (muthannā): Dual grammatical number; here used to argue that the dual (like 'two days') is treated like the plural.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows i'tikaf for three days but does not mention nights, what is required?
A) He must do three days only
B) He must also do the intervening three nights
C) He must do three nights only
D) He may choose days or nights

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a vow of i'tikaf for any number of days obligates the corresponding nights and vice versa, due to the inherent continuity of the worship.
